The book is not just about math but is actually about how to solve problems – any problems – and it became the foundation for the field of heuristics.\n\n## Four-Step Problem-Solving Framework\n\nThe book suggests the following steps when solving a mathematical problem: First, you have to understand the problem. After understanding, make a plan. Carry out the plan. Look back on your work.\n\n## Key Principles\n\nPólya's approach centers on three fundamental principles:\n\n1. Active learning, believing students learn best by discovering solutions themselves rather than being told answers.\n\n2. Heuristic thinking - the art of guided discovery and learning from experience, viewing problem-solving as a skill that can be developed through practice.\n\n3. Sophisticated questioning techniques that guide students toward solutions while encouraging independent thinking.\n\n## Key Heuristic Strategies\n\nThe book includes a heuristic dictionary with dozens of entries on how to make problems more manageable—from analogy and induction to the heuristic method of starting with a goal and working backward to something you already know.\n\nSpecific strategies include:\n\n- Analogy: Find similar problems and patterns, drawing on past experience.\n- Decomposition: Break complex problems into smaller, manageable parts.\n- Generalization and Specialization: Identify broad principles while testing specific cases.\n- Working Backwards: Start from the desired outcome and trace steps backward.\n- Auxiliary Elements: Add helpful constructions, diagrams, notation, or intermediate goals.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"79070b53-b312-47e2-8662-0632c714e1b5","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:31:27.423Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"017d9913-9b7b-40d9-9271-cc09d66915fa","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

Hardy's \"A Mathematician's Apology\":\n\n## Core Thesis\n\nA Mathematician's Apology is a 1940 essay by British mathematician G. H. Hardy which defends the pursuit of mathematics for its own sake, with a central argument that mathematics has value independent of its applications.\n\n## Aesthetics of Mathematics\n\nOne of the main themes of the book is the beauty that mathematics possesses, which Hardy compares to painting and poetry. \"A mathematician, like a painter or poet, is a maker of patterns. If his patterns are more permanent than theirs, it is because they are made with ideas... The mathematician's patterns, like the painter's or the poet's must be beautiful; the ideas like the colours or the words, must fit together in a harmonious way.\"\n\n## Pure vs. Applied Mathematics\n\nFor Hardy, the most beautiful mathematics was that which had no practical applications (pure mathematics) and, in particular number theory, Hardy's own field. Hardy denigrates much of the applied mathematics as either being \"trivial\", \"ugly\", or \"dull\" and contrasts it with \"real mathematics\", which is how he describes pure mathematics.\n\n## Defining Mathematical Beauty\n\nThe beauty of a result is intimately linked with its significance. But there's also a purely aesthetic aspect to a proof. Aesthetically, such theorems involve unexpectedness, inevitability, and economy.\n\n## Writing and the Mathematician\n\n\"Exposition, criticism, appreciation, is work for second-rate minds... It is a melancholy experience for a professional mathematician to find himself writing about mathematics. The function of a mathematician is to do something, to prove new theorems, to add to mathematics, and not to talk about what he or other mathematicians have done.\"\n\n## Mathematics as a Young Person's Endeavor\n\nAnother theme is that mathematics is a \"young man's game\". Hardy believed that anyone with a talent for mathematics should develop and use that talent while they are young, before their ability to create original mathematics starts to decline in middle age.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5460f6e5-d818-478d-8c42-27f77b855ab1","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:31:29.025Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"f6ba7372-aa78-43a1-b0b3-6bdf12966df8","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

The first edition was written by William Strunk Jr. in 1918, and E. B. White revised and expanded it for publication in 1959.\n\n## Key Writing Principles\n\n**Omit Needless Words**\nStrunk's recommendation: \"Vigorous writing is concise. A sentence should contain no unnecessary words, a paragraph no unnecessary sentences, for the same reason that a drawing should have no unnecessary lines and a machine no unnecessary parts. This requires not that the writer make all his sentences short, or that he avoid all detail and treat his subjects only in outline, but that he make every word tell.\"\n\n**Use Active Voice**\nUse the active voice. Active voice instantly shows who did what, and it propels narratives and arguments forward.\n\n**Additional Core Principles**\nThe 11 Elementary Principles of Composition include: choose a suitable design and stick to it, make the paragraph the unit of composition, put statements in positive form, and use definite, specific, concrete language.\n\nExpress coordinate ideas in similar form—known as parallelism—ensuring that ideas of equal importance are presented in a consistent grammatical structure.\n\n## Style and Clarity\n\nGood writing often requires multiple drafts, and Strunk and White emphasize the importance of revision to improve clarity, coherence, and style. Above all, clarity is paramount—writers should aim to express their ideas in a way that is easily understood by readers.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"1c113661-f76c-4729-84c4-e89c9abdef3d","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:31:48.551Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"71fc5837-5711-4fd8-8bc9-362f7fea4f30","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

Here's a summary of the search results:\n\n## Leslie Lamport's \"How to Write a Proof\"\n\nThe search successfully located Leslie Lamport's influential paper on structured proofs. The method proposes writing proofs that makes it much harder to prove things that are not true, using hierarchical structuring as a simple and practical approach.\n\n### Key Advice on Structured Proofs:\n\nStructured proofs are longer than conventional ones, including more detail, which makes it obvious when steps have been forgotten or important details omitted. They make it hard to be sloppy—the assertion \"this case is similar to the previous one\" is not acceptable; one is forced to find the appropriate general step that makes the proof of both cases easy.\n\n### Effectiveness:\n\nLamport discovered that when colleagues wrote proofs in a structured style, almost every one had serious mistakes initially, though the theorems were correct. Time and again, conjectures that could easily have been turned into convincing conventional proofs were found to be false when attempting to write structured proofs.\n\n### Reader Reception:\n\nReaders' reactions to structured proofs have been mixed—skeptical readers who check for errors like these proofs much more than conventional ones.\n\nThe paper is available as a PDF at the Azure website mentioned in the search results and remains an important reference for mathematical proof writing.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"704d5f97-f550-4978-b05b-712b472a82de","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:32:06.109Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"f480f209-65a8-4d5e-a553-febe894ab497","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

McCloskey shows that good writing is not just a matter of taste—it's a product of adept intuition and a rigorous revision process.\n\n## Key Principles\n\n**Clarity Over Impression**\n\n\"If you write to impress, it will fail.\" McCloskey insists that you focus on clarity, not on big words. Writers should strive to present their ideas in a straightforward manner, avoiding jargon and complex syntax that can obscure meaning.\n\n**Omit Unnecessary Words**\n\n\"The best sentence is one that contains no unnecessary words.\" Inspired by Strunk & White, McCloskey constantly reminds us to trim the fat from our sentences.\n\n**Use Active Voice**\n\nEffective writing begins with the careful use of strong, active verbs. Passive constructions can obscure the subject and muddle the action, while active verbs bring clarity and vigor to your sentences.\n\n**Revision is Essential**\n\nMcCloskey stresses the value of revision. The course was given at Stanford University during autumn quarter, 1987.\n\n## Key Topics Covered\n\nThe lecture notes covered several important areas of mathematical writing:\n\n- How to avoid excessive subscripts and superscripts; discussion of the documentation of algorithms, computer programs, and user manuals; and consideration of the process of refereeing and editing\n- How to make effective diagrams and tables, and how to find appropriate quotations to spice up a text\n\n## Notable Guest Lecturers\n\nAmong the 31 lectures are guest appearances by Herb Wilf, Jeff Ullman, Leslie Lamport, Nils Nilsson, Mary-Claire van Leunen, Rosalie Stemer, and Paul Halmos.\n\n## Advice on Writing Style\n\nOne notable principle covered concerns pronoun usage: Knuth says that Mary-Claire van Leunen defends the use of 'I' in scholarly articles, but that he disagrees (unless the identity of the author is important to the reader). Halrnos | Springer Nature Link (formerly SpringerLink)\",\"url\":\"https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/978-3-0346-0347-8_4.pdf\"}]\n\nBased on the web search results, I can provide you with information about Halmos's \"How to Write Mathematics\" and its core principles.\n\n## Core Principles\n\nThe basic problem in writing mathematics is to communicate an idea, requiring that you have something to say, someone to say it to, organize what you want to say, arrange it in the desired order, write and rewrite it several times, and be willing to work hard on mechanical details such as diction, notation, and punctuation.\n\nHalmos expands on this foundation with several key principles:\n\n### \"Say Something\"\nTo have something to say is by far the most important ingredient of good exposition. This means don't say nothing, and don't say lots of things.\n\n### \"Speak to Someone\" (Write for Someone)\nThe second principle of good writing is to write for someone. Halmos recommends specifying the audience in both a vague, large sense and in a very specific, personal sense, keeping in mind a particular person as you write.\n\n### \"Organize\"\nArrange the material so as to minimize the resistance and maximize the insight of the reader.\n\n## Additional Key Elements\n\nThe search results also reveal other important principles from Halmos:\n\n- Use consistent notation, with careful design of the letters or symbols denoting the concepts discussed.\n- Write in spirals by writing and rewriting sections progressively rather than completing one section entirely before moving to the next.\n- Use words correctly, thinking carefully about both common logical words and technical mathematical terms that affect mathematical meaning.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"ffe060ad-e8d9-4435-9063-89fad2efe8dc","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:33:05.048Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"461bc8c4-8d2b-4bd5-a85d-aa6fbe29850d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

distinguish ∈ from ε).\n\n5. **Write in Spirals** - Draft chapters multiple times in increasing depth, allowing early versions to be rough.\n\n6. **Maintain Organizational Consistency** - Ensure later sections have the examples and concepts introduced earlier.\n\n7. **Use Clear, Unobtrusive Language** - Write correct English that doesn't draw attention to itself; avoid both casual laziness and flowery pedantry.\n\n8. **Be Honest About Clarity** - Question whether \"obvious\" statements truly are; test by explaining to others later.\n\n9. **Eliminate Unnecessary Content** - Address trivial cases but don't dwell on them; keep theorems self-contained without extra hypotheses.\n\n10. **Balance Repetition** - Use identical wording when statements are nearly identical; vary wording if repeating for understanding.\n\n11. **Use \"We\" Appropriately** - The editorial \"we\" means \"writer and reader,\" not royal \"we.\"\n\n12. **Master Technical Language** - Use terms consistently and correctly; While some mathematicians advise writing it last, writing the Introduction first helps you organize your thoughts.\n\n### Getting to Your Point Quickly\n\nGet to the purpose of your paper as soon as possible and don't begin with a pile of notation. Even at the risk of being less technical, inform readers of the purpose of your paper as soon as you can, as readers want to know immediately if they're interested in reading your paper.\n\nThe introduction to an article should get to new and interesting theorems as soon as possible. It is OK to postpone definitions to a later \"Notation\" section if those definitions are standard enough that most readers will be able to read the introduction without them.\n\n### Contextualizing Your Framework\n\nExplain the history that's directly relevant to your main result. For example, show how previous papers asked about related problems and what they proved or disproved, then explain how your work refines existing tools.\n\n### Structure at Multiple Scales\n\nAt the whole-paper scale, the introduction sets the context, the results are the content, and the discussion brings home the conclusion. At the paragraph scale, the first sentence defines the topic or context, the body hosts the novel content, and the last sentence provides the conclusion to be remembered.\n\n### Clarity Over Notation\n\nDefinitions for terms and notations used throughout the paper must be set at the beginning, and it is more effective to include this within the Introduction section rather than having a stand-alone section of definitions.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"c5e1cca6-bf0c-467d-b8be-9326e40efe91","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:33:38.510Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"989cd34d-be2e-4686-a8ed-daa1786d04ad","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

Lakatos's purpose is to challenge formalism in mathematics, and to show that informal mathematics grows by a logic of \"proofs and refutations\".\n\n## Core Argument\n\nLakatos is concerned throughout to combat the classical picture of mathematical development as a steady accumulation of established truths. He shows that mathematics grows instead through a richer, more dramatic process of the successive improvement of creative hypotheses by attempts to 'prove' them and by criticism of these attempts: the logic of proofs and refutations.\n\n## Mathematical Knowledge as Fallible and Dynamic\n\nLakatos was after an account of informal mathematics as a fallible dynamic body of knowledge. Rejecting the positivist distinction between context of discovery and context of justification, he claimed that mathematical practice and its history are not the domain of the irrational but rather display an objectivity and rationality that any philosophy of mathematics worth its name should account for.\n\n## The Method: Proofs and Refutations\n\nLakatos summarizes his method by the following stages: Primitive conjecture. Proof (a rough thought-experiment or argument, decomposing the primitive conjecture into subconjectures). \"Global\" counterexamples (counterexamples to the primitive conjecture) emerge. Proof re-examined: the \"guilty lemma\" to which the global counter-example is a \"local\" counterexample is spotted. The hitherto accepted consequences of the original and now refuted conjecture are checked. Counterexamples are turned into new examples - new fields of inquiry open up.\n\n## Definitions and Concepts\n\nA central theme is that definitions are not carved in stone, but often have to be patched up in the light of later insights, in particular failed proofs. This gives mathematics a somewhat experimental flavour.\n\n## Format and Structure\n\nThe book is written as a series of Socratic dialogues involving a group of students who debate the proof of the Euler characteristic defined for the polyhedron.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"764425eb-7d68-4488-be2b-2c3dde1f4c7a","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:33:41.008Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"162345ed-fc55-4763-b761-6f90b3e7fcb5","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

Be Clear**\n- Clarity is the foundational principle for mathematical writing\n- Understanding what clarity means and why it matters precedes all other considerations\n- \"For the sake of clarity, ignore all rules\" suggests clarity supersedes style conventions\n\n**2. Macro-Level Structure**\nThe paper should include:\n- **Title**: Clear and descriptive\n- **Abstract**: Concise overview\n- **Table of contents**: Helps readers navigate\n- **Introduction**: Sets context and motivation\n- **Foreword**: Additional framing (when needed)\n- **Main body**: Organized logically\n- **Final Remarks**: Concluding thoughts\n- **Acknowledgements**: Credit contributors\n- **References**: Properly formatted citations\n\n**3. References are Critical**\n- Understand *why* citations matter for credibility and context\n- Learn to cite individual papers correctly\n- Master citing multiple papers together\n- Know *where* to place citations within text\n- Form references properly with complete information\n- Maintain consistent reference style throughout\n\n**4. Micro-Level Polish**\n- Master basic grammar, syntax, and punctuation\n- Avoid being overly pedantic or pretentious\n- \"Downshift your style\" to increase accessibility\n- Use LaTeX effectively but verify outputs manually\n- Don't trust LaTeX formatting blindly\n- Create clear, well-labeled figures\n\n**5. Post-Completion Steps**\n- Recognize writing is never truly finished\n- Advertise and popularize your work\n- Revise substantially and republish when appropriate\n\n## Specific Technical Tips\n\n- Read quality nonfiction writing guides first\n- Study literature in your field before writing\n- Structure papers logically for reader comprehension\n- Use consistent notation and definitions\n- Make figures informative and properly integrated\n- Proofread multiple times for errors LaTeX may miss\n\nThis framework emphasizes that clarity and logical organization serve as the foundation for all mathematical writing, with meticulous attention to both macro structure and micro details.\n\n[Binary content (application/pdf, 402.3KB) also saved to /Users/wisdomhappy/.claude/projects/-Users-wisdomhappy/538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221/tool-results/webfetch-1774960427352-js74y5.pdf]"}]},"uuid":"c867a729-7dfa-4beb-89bc-2410aa568b73","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:33:55.461Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4f26123a-6bc6-48ba-b955-62f792807f02","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

{"parentUuid":"8b03a849-56e1-4e51-8fc0-c4b0c962764f","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"681c93ef-0530-48e9-91f8-eae0112ab083","agentId":"a7d9da8a1dfc89850","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_015PbQNjUvm2dSYc8HaKKZxf","type":"tool_result","content":"# Ten Simple Rules for Structuring Papers\n\n**Rule 1: Focus on One Central Contribution**\nConcentrate your paper on a single main message communicated through the title. \"Papers that simultaneously focus on multiple contributions tend to be less convincing about each and are therefore less memorable.\"\n\n**Rule 2: Write for Naive Readers**\nConsider your audience as people unfamiliar with your work. Define technical terms clearly, avoid unnecessary abbreviations, and minimize cognitive load by keeping loose threads to a minimum.\n\n**Rule 3: Use Context-Content-Conclusion Structure**\nOrganize information so readers understand why material matters (context), what you discovered (content), and what it means (conclusion). This structure applies across all scales—entire papers, paragraphs, and sentences.\n\n**Rule 4: Optimize Logical Flow**\nAvoid jumping between unrelated topics. Present related ideas consecutively and use parallel structure when presenting similar points to enhance readability.\n\n**Rule 5: Tell a Complete Story in the Abstract**\nYour abstract must convey the entire paper's message using the full C-C-C structure, starting broad, narrowing to your specific gap, then broadening again with significance.\n\n**Rule 6: Communicate Why the Paper Matters in the Introduction**\nEstablish progressively specific gaps in current knowledge, culminating in a clear statement of what your paper will contribute. The final paragraph summarizes your results.\n\n**Rule 7: Present Results as Logically Connected Statements**\nSequence results according to the logical structure supporting your claim. Use clear headers and ensure figures communicate conclusions, as \"figures are often viewed by readers who skip directly from the abstract.\"\n\n**Rule 8: Discuss Limitations and Field Relevance**\nAddress how your results fill identified gaps, acknowledge limitations, and explain how your contribution advances the field and enables future research.\n\n**Rule 9: Allocate Time Strategically**\nInvest effort proportionally—titles, abstracts, and figures receive far more attention than methods sections. Create detailed outlines before writing to prevent wasted effort on unsuitable paragraphs.\n\n**Rule 10: Iterate Through Feedback**\nTreat writing as optimization requiring multiple reviewers. \"Successfully writing a paper typically requires input from multiple people\" who can identify whether your central contribution comes through clearly."}]},"uuid":"083c5a05-a53f-42a9-9a1d-88d48610868f","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:33:56.725Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"8b03a849-56e1-4e51-8fc0-c4b0c962764f","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

Tufte claims that good graphical representations maximize data-ink and erase as much non-data-ink as possible.\n\n## Key Principles\n\n**1. Above All Else Show the Data**\nThe principle can be summarized—in Tufte's own words—by one of the five principles in the theory of data graphics: Above all else show the data.\n\n**2. Maximize Data-Ink and Minimize Non-Data-Ink**\nData-ink is that portion of ink dedicated to represent the core of a graph—the measured quantities. Non-data-ink is ink used to draw unnecessary elements of a chart that do not contribute to clarifying the intended message.\n\n**3. Eliminate Chartjunk**\nChartjunk is a term coined by Tufte to describe all the unnecessary or distracting elements in a data visualization that do not contribute to understanding the information being presented.\n\n**4. Data Density and Clarity**\nThe book advocates for dense data graphics, showing that well-designed charts can display a wealth of information in a small space. Tufte emphasizes the importance of creating clear and concise graphics that communicate information efficiently.\n\n**5. Graphical Integrity**\nThe book underscores the need to maintain the integrity of data when visualizing it, warning against misrepresenting or distorting information.\n\nThese principles remain foundational to modern data visualization practice.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"348fcfcb-ae54-44b3-bc38-85f7d7b5432b","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:34:13.327Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"bb054b05-b732-4296-ae27-0650576ffd2d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

Scientists are particularly skeptical when a new theory is mainly theoretical and lacks sufficient evidence.\n\n## Key Reasons for Reviewer Skepticism\n\nResistance to scientific theories is often because humans resist change, and scientists may be unwilling to give up on their existing research and work to make space for new theories. When an innovation threatens orthodoxy, the establishment tends to ignore, ridicule, or resist it.\n\n## How to Strengthen Your Paper\n\n1. **Provide Robust Evidence**: A new theory needs evidence and explanations to be accepted. Great ideas sometimes arrive too early, needing new data, technology, or infrastructure to be fully realized, and breakthroughs can remain invisible until enough context and evidence accumulates.\n\n2. **Build on Existing Knowledge**: An idea can be ignored when it lacks an audience or fits no accepted theory at the time. Consider how your theory connects to existing research streams.\n\n3. **Expect Conservative Scrutiny**: The majority of the scientific community will oppose any conceptual change, which is appropriate. A scientific community needs to contain both individuals who are bold and individuals who are conservative.\n\n4. **Leverage Practical Demonstration**: One strategy that worked historically was taking conference attendees to a lab to see experiments firsthand, which convinced skeptics.\n\n5. **Support from Leadership**: Consensual processes like peer review should be supplemented by leadership from those with courage to back innovative work, requiring support from the top of scientific management and funding.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"50f02243-7c86-480d-a8bc-01eed0220edd","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:34:16.331Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1d946288-1ed6-4485-b7bb-966c66791676","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

The book was published by the American Mathematical Society on December 1, 1973.\n\n## Key Principles by Author\n\n**Steenrod's Contribution:**\nSteenrod draws a clear distinction between formal and informal content, addressing important issues of global and local organization for each. More specifically, he distinguishes between the formal or logical structure consisting of definitions, theorems, and proofs, and the complementary informal or introductory material consisting of motivations, analogies, examples, and metamathematical explanations, emphasizing that this division should be conspicuously maintained because the logical structure must be clear.\n\n**Halmos's Contribution:**\nHalmos' essay is by far the most thorough, covering everything from content to audience to organization and writing (he advocates an interesting O(n^2) \"spiral\" writing algorithm) to fine points of language and symbolism.\n\n**Schiffer's Contribution:**\nSchiffer examines differences between several types of expository writing: research paper, monograph, survey, and textbook.\n\n**Dieudonné's Contribution:**\nDieudonné's essay is the only disappointment, consisting of three short paragraphs of remarks about the previous articles.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d0e0c840-cf21-4814-b60f-a092d151b69e","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:34:16.351Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c903a10a-6cef-47a9-a224-2bb6949b7d55","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

Prove equivalence? Connect unrelated fields? Apply new methods to old problems? Provide new proofs? Classify structures? Resolve special cases?\n\n**Understanding Internal Organization:**\n- Determine if results concentrate in one theorem or multiple related ones\n- Identify counterexamples and their role\n- Clarify whether work is purely theoretical or involves multiple approaches\n\n**Standard Paper Sections:**\n1. **Background** - Orient readers with problem history, explain why the question matters, warn of prerequisites\n2. **Introduction** - Transition from big picture to specific results; introduce necessary definitions\n3. **Body** - Present main work across multiple sections\n4. **Future Work/Implications** - Direct future research; discuss real-world consequences if applicable\n\n## Section 3: Formal vs. Informal Exposition\n\n**Maintain Clear Division:**\n- \"Formal structure\" = definitions, theorems, proofs\n- \"Informal structure\" = motivations, analogies, examples, explanations\n\n**Formal Structure Strategies:**\n- Develop logical outline before writing prose\n- Name results appropriately (lemmas as foundations, theorems as substance, corollaries as extensions)\n- Seek common lemmas: if proofs repeat techniques, extract underlying lemma\n- Recognize that research rarely follows linear proof structure—arrange presentation to reflect actual logical dependencies\n\n**Informal Structure:**\n- Explain \"why\" statements are true and how discoveries occurred\n- Recognize people think logically but rarely think in pure logic\n\n**Notation Selection:**\n- Notation is a language readers must learn\n- Follow conventions unless there's compelling reason not to\n- Avoid non-standard symbols (using epsilon for prime integers, unusual function notation)\n\n## Section 4: Writing Proofs\n\n**Theorem Statement:**\n- Include exactly necessary hypotheses—no more, no fewer\n- Remove extraneous assumptions that distract from main point\n\n**Proof Organization:**\n- Map hypotheses and deductions before writing\n- Order statements to minimize reader confusion\n\n**Proof Writing Techniques:**\n\n1. **Write Proofs Forward** - Begin with starting assumptions and proceed step-by-step to conclusion, rather than reverse-engineering from desired result\n\n2. **Avoid Unnecessary Notation** - Replace equation-chain proofs with explanatory prose describing the strategy: \"substitute p for q, collect terms, permute factors...\" rather than showing only results\n\n## Section 5: Specific Recommendations\n\n**Notation & Symbols:**\n- Remind readers of notation not used recently (within paragraphs/pages)\n- Never use same symbol for multiple purposes\n- Avoid unused symbols entirely: \"no letter only once\"\n- All notation must be meaningful with no free variables\n- Never start sentences with symbols; use word form instead\n\n**Punctuation & Structure:**\n- Structure should be \"easily discernible by headings and punctuation\"\n- Don't overwork small punctuation marks—they're easily overlooked\n- Use \"then\" with \"if\" in mathematical contexts; absence creates confusion\n- Avoid ambiguous \"any\"—replace with \"each\" or \"every\"\n- Avoid vague \"where\" clauses that indicate lazy afterthoughts\n- Don't use \"equivalent\" for theorems (logical nonsense)\n- Eliminate \"if...then...if...then\" constructions; recast as single conditional\n\n**Page Architecture/Layout:**\n- Balance prose with displays: avoid both solid prose and symbol-heavy hash\n- Intersperse enough white space and formatting for visual relief\n- Break up content meaningfully without excess\n\n**Clarity Techniques:**\n- Maintain clear problem definition throughout\n- Use counterexamples to demonstrate necessity of theorem conditions\n- Distinguish carefully between functions and their values\n- Craft titles communicating substance to both experts and novices\n- Use technical terminology Here are the key principles and advice from his work:\n\n## Key Principles for Mathematical Writing\n\n**Clarity and Justification:**\nIf a claim does not follow immediately from the previous sentence alone, explain what it does follow from. Additionally, if a sentence contains more than one claim, make clear which reason is the justification for each claim. For example, in a chain of equalities, make clear which reason explains which equality.\n\n**Reader Understanding:**\nA reader reaching the period of a sentence should know why each claim up to that point is true.\n\n**Grammar and Structure:**\nFiller words like \"We remark that\" and \"Note that\" should be omitted, except when their absence would produce a sentence starting with a symbol. Also, two sentences can be connected by a semicolon, but not just a comma; alternatively, a colon can be used if the second sentence explains the first. Adverbs like \"therefore\", \"hence\", and \"thus\" do not change this, but coordinating conjunctions like \"and\", \"but\", and \"so\" can join two sentences with a preceding comma.\n\n**Overall Approach:**\nThe primary goal of an article is to bring the reader on a rewarding mathematical journey. Your role as a writer is analogous to that of a leader of a hike—you need to gently guide your readers through the weeds of a technical argument while making sure they fully appreciate the highlights of your work.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5fe7d07d-4c1f-4d87-b878-61457dff8f0d","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:34:32.562Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"20d54c75-6d0e-4d77-b678-3cdb5be419be","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

As one example shows, early flatworm memory studies couldn't be reliably replicated, creating lasting skepticism.\n\n3. **Paradigm conflicts** - Ideas contradicting current models get dismissed. \"Experts had concluded it was fundamentally impossible\" to achieve certain laser effects, yet it happened anyway.\n\n4. **Wrong reasoning, right conclusion** - When researchers propose correct findings through flawed logic, peers reject both the conclusion and the mechanism.\n\n5. **Scientific flip-flopping** - Once rejected, ideas become difficult to resurrect. Cancer immunotherapy faced 40+ years of neglect after initial dismissal.\n\n## Practical Lessons for New Paradigm Advocates\n\n**Publication strategies:**\n- Expect 5-15 journal rejections before acceptance (mRNA vaccines, CRISPR, PCR all faced this)\n- Submit to specialized journals when prestigious ones reject you\n- Persevere; alternatives exist even if Nature/Science say no\n\n**Funding survival:**\n- Build diverse funding sources (private foundations saved expansion microscopy research)\n- Expect NIH rejections; Ed Boyden faced 9 of 10 grant rejections initially\n\n**Credibility building:**\n- Recruit established allies within the field\n- Replicate findings rigorously to overcome historical skepticism\n- Accept partial credit rather than claiming to revolutionize everything\n\n**Tactical positioning:**\n- Work at periphery institutions if needed—freedom from trends matters\n- Frame work conservatively initially, expand implications later\n- Document early rejection; it becomes validation after success"}]},"uuid":"f6091df9-a1fe-4ec3-85c7-0ae57f56eb71","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:34:33.840Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"cf303094-2fda-40d0-84f0-09974dc96513","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

The author emphasizes that successful math teachers often fail with general audiences because they focus on explaining *how* to do math, whereas lay readers need engaging narratives instead.\n\n## The One-Point Strategy\n\"Write with the assumption that when your reader stops reading your article they will leave having assimilated just one point.\" Focus on a single overarching message about mathematics' nature or relevance—not specific facts or calculations.\n\n## Narrative Structure\n**Tell stories about people**, not abstract concepts. Feature individuals or small groups as exemplars, since human brains are evolutionarily drawn to interpersonal narratives. Create mental imagery through vivid language rather than diagrams or formulas.\n\n## Critical Constraints\n- Avoid opening with puzzles, which alienate non-puzzle-lovers\n- Never include calculations or mathematical formulas in main text\n- Don't teach, explain procedures, or present logical arguments\n- Lead with an intriguing anecdote that establishes your main point immediately\n\n## Target Audiences\nDevlin identifies three practical audiences: K-12 teachers, parents of students, and education policymakers—people seeking accessible information during leisure time.\n\n## Essential Mindset Shift\nStop functioning as a teacher; become a storyteller instead."}]},"uuid":"74abebdd-7a8a-4f83-9098-feca277692b2","timestamp":"2026-03-31T12:34:34.761Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"85fb8352-3879-4034-8648-f60400c7c4e1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"claude-vscode","cwd":"/Users/wisdomhappy","sessionId":"538815ea-43d5-439c-86f4-2676ed06e221","version":"2.1.88","gitBranch":"HEAD","slug":"humble-crafting-sloth"}

{"parentUuid":"bdb89aee-9cec-4a72-bf0a-ee4fdd1d67c5","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a7d9da8a1dfc89850","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_01NVfBCaWqYZzmqGBCEP6Xvj","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_01QX28MxZvka1F1pdUyhtx2B","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/Users/wisdomhappy/Playful Sincerity/PS Research/IVNA/research/findings/books-writing-research-papers.md","content":"# Books on Writing Exceptional Research Papers\n## Research Findings — 2026-03-31\n\nThis document catalogs books and resources on writing mathematically rigorous, philosophically compelling, and paradigm-shifting research papers. Focused on: mathematical writing craft, philosophy of discovery, academic persuasion, and presenting novel frameworks to skeptical audiences.\n\n---\n\n## Category A: Mathematical Writing Craft\n\n### 1. \"How to Write Mathematics\" — Paul R. Halmos (1970)\n**Published in:** L'Enseignement Mathematique, Vol. 16  \n**Free PDF sources:**\n- https://www.math.lsu.edu/~oxley/halmos.pdf\n- https://sites.stat.columbia.edu/liam/teaching/HalmosWrite.pdf\n- https://entropiesschool.sciencesconf.org/data/How_to_Write_Mathematics.pdf\n\n**Why it matters:** The canonical essay on mathematical exposition. Halmos covers everything from content to audience to organization to fine points of language and symbolism. The most cited guide in the mathematical community.\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n1. Have something to say — \"To have something to say is by far the most important ingredient of good exposition.\" Don't say nothing, don't say everything.\n2. Write for someone specific — Identify your audience in both a general sense (e.g., second-year graduate students) and a specific, personal sense (imagine a particular person you've discussed the subject with).\n3. Organize — \"Arrange the material so as to minimize the resistance and maximize the insight of the reader.\" Create an outline before writing anything.\n4. Choose notation carefully — Avoid confusing symbols; don't use a letter for two different purposes; design the notation system before writing.\n5. Write in spirals — Draft chapters multiple times in increasing depth. Early versions are rough; later passes refine. This O(n^2) algorithm prevents local over-polish at the expense of global coherence.\n6. Use \"we\" correctly — The editorial \"we\" means \"writer and reader together,\" not the royal \"we.\" It invites the reader into the journey.\n7. Minimize symbols — Prefer words to symbols wherever natural English works equally well. A sentence should be grammatically complete.\n8. Never begin a sentence with a symbol — Write \"The function f...\" not \"f is...\"\n9. Be honest about \"obvious\" — Question every use of \"clearly\" or \"obviously.\" If it requires thought, show the thought.\n10. Stop when done — Do not pad after making your point.\n\n**Full AMS Collection:** \"How to Write Mathematics\" (1973) — Steenrod, Halmos, Schiffer, Dieudonne  \nAvailable at: https://bookstore.ams.org/HWM  \nThis four-essay collection covers: formal vs. informal structure (Steenrod), writing craft (Halmos), types of expository writing (Schiffer), and overview (Dieudonne). Steenrod's distinction between \"formal structure\" (definitions, theorems, proofs) and \"informal structure\" (motivations, analogies, examples) is particularly useful — he argues this division must be conspicuously maintained because the logical structure must be clear.\n\n---\n\n### 2. \"Mathematical Writing\" — Knuth, Larrabee, Roberts (1989)\n**Publisher:** Mathematical Association of America (MAA Notes, No. 14)  \n**Free PDF sources:**\n- https://jmlr.csail.mit.edu/reviewing-papers/knuth_mathematical_writing.pdf\n- https://www-cs-faculty.stanford.edu/~knuth/papers/cs1193.pdf (Stanford official report)\n\n**Why it matters:** Transcript of Knuth's 1987 Stanford course on mathematical writing, featuring guest lectures from Paul Halmos, Leslie Lamport, Herb Wilf, and others. Covers everything from prose style to algorithm documentation to the process of refereeing. One of the most comprehensive and concrete guides available.\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- Avoid excessive subscripts and superscripts — they create visual noise and slow reading\n- Use \"we\" to mean \"author and reader together\" — it creates collaborative engagement\n- The primary goal is to \"bring the reader on a rewarding mathematical journey\" (Poonen, building on Knuth)\n- Learn to create effective diagrams and tables — visual communication is part of mathematical exposition\n- Study the literature in your field before writing — you must know conventions to depart from them consciously\n- Documentation of algorithms requires the same care as proofs — each step must be justified\n\n---\n\n### 3. \"A Primer of Mathematical Writing\" — Steven G. Krantz (2nd ed., 2017)\n**Publisher:** American Mathematical Society  \n**arXiv preprint:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1612.04888\n\n**Why it matters:** Practical, comprehensive guide covering grammar, syntax, usage, TeX typesetting, email etiquette, publishing, refereeing, and all aspects of academic mathematical life. Good for concrete technical advice.\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- Don't agonize over each word while writing a first draft — get ideas on the page; agonize during editing\n- Never use \"very\" unless you'd be comfortable using \"damn\" in its place (tests whether intensification is warranted)\n- Don't use the same word, or even the same sound, in two consecutive sentences — creates unintentional rhythm\n- A good mathematical sentence is complete, with subject, verb, and object\n- Define terms at first use; never assume the reader shares your notation conventions\n\n---\n\n### 4. \"Handbook of Writing for the Mathematical Sciences\" — Nicholas J. Higham (3rd ed., 2020)\n**Publisher:** SIAM  \n**Author's page:** https://nhigham.com/handbook-of-writing-for-the-mathematical-sciences/\n\n**Why it matters:** Comprehensive modern handbook covering everything from general writing principles through mathematical typesetting, publishing, giving talks, and writing theses. Incorporates modern digital publishing. A full-spectrum professional reference.\n\n**Topics covered (from table of contents):**\n- General principles of scientific writing\n- Mathematical writing (notation, equations, symbols vs. words)\n- English usage and grammar\n- Writing when English is a foreign language\n- Writing a paper from scratch\n- Revising a draft\n- Publishing a paper\n- Writing and defending a thesis\n- Writing a talk / giving a talk / preparing a poster\n- TeX and LaTeX\n\n---\n\n### 5. \"Practical Suggestions for Mathematical Writing\" — Bjorn Poonen (MIT)\n**Free PDF:** https://math.mit.edu/~poonen/papers/writing.pdf  \n**Also in:** AMS Notices, June 2021\n\n**Why it matters:** Concise, densely practical guide from a leading number theorist. Excellent on sentence-level clarity and proof structure.\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- \"The primary goal of an article is to bring the reader on a rewarding mathematical journey.\" The writer is a guide, not a gatekeeper.\n- A reader reaching the period of a sentence should know why each claim up to that point is true\n- If a claim does not follow from the previous sentence alone, say what it does follow from\n- In a chain of equalities, make clear which reason justifies which equality\n- Omit filler phrases like \"We remark that\" and \"Note that\" — except when needed to prevent a sentence from starting with a symbol\n- Two sentences can be connected by a semicolon; not just a comma\n- Adverbs like \"therefore\" and \"hence\" do not join sentences — conjunctions like \"and,\" \"but,\" and \"so\" do (with a preceding comma)\n\n---\n\n### 6. \"How to Write Mathematical Papers\" — Bruce C. Berndt\n**Free PDF:** https://math.illinois.edu/sites/default/files/2025-04/Berndt_writingmath.pdf\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- The title should have no more than ten words and be informative — \"On a conjecture of Daisy Dud\" is useless unless everyone knows who she is\n- The Introduction is the most important part of your paper — Berndt advocates writing it first to organize thinking\n- Get to the purpose of the paper as soon as possible; don't begin with notation piles\n- Every non-trivial step should be documented and steps should be related in sentences\n- Display complicated expressions rather than embedding them in text\n- \"Your paper should be attractive, neat, and beautiful — this puts editors and referees in a good mood\"\n\n---\n\n### 7. \"How to Write a Clear Math Paper: Some 21st Century Tips\" — Igor Pak (UCLA)\n**Free PDF:** https://www.math.ucla.edu/~pak/papers/how-to-write1.pdf\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- Clarity is the foundational principle — \"For the sake of clarity, ignore all rules\"\n- Include a table of contents for longer papers\n- Understand why citations matter, not just how to format them — citations situate your work in context\n- \"Downshift your style\" — write at one level below your natural register to increase accessibility\n- Avoid being pedantic or pretentious — it undermines the mathematics\n- Read quality nonfiction writing guides before starting — mathematical writing skills transfer from general writing\n- Recognize that writing is never truly finished — revise substantially and republish when appropriate\n- Advertise and popularize your work after publication\n\n---\n\n### 8. \"Writing a Research Paper in Mathematics\" — J. Rickert (MIT)\n**Free web page:** https://web.mit.edu/jrickert/www/mathadvice.html\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- \"Written communication acts as a filter through which your mathematical work is viewed\"\n- Before writing, ask: Does your result strengthen previous work? Prove equivalence? Connect unrelated fields? Apply new methods to old problems? Classify structures? Resolve special cases? — the answer shapes the framing\n- Formal structure = definitions, theorems, proofs; Informal structure = motivations, analogies, examples, explanations — both are required\n- Develop logical outline before writing prose\n- Name results appropriately: lemmas as foundations, theorems as substance, corollaries as extensions\n- Extract common lemmas — if two proofs repeat techniques, there's an underlying general lemma\n- Write proofs forward — begin with hypotheses and proceed to conclusion, do not reverse-engineer\n- Avoid \"any\" — replace with \"each\" or \"every\" to remove ambiguity\n- Avoid \"where\" clauses — they indicate a lazy afterthought; restructure the sentence\n- Do not use \"equivalent\" for theorems unless you mean logical equivalence\n\n---\n\n### 9. \"How to Write a Proof\" — Leslie Lamport (1993)\n**Free PDF:** https://lamport.azurewebsites.net/pubs/lamport-how-to-write.pdf\n\n**Why it matters:** Lamport proposes hierarchically structured proofs that make each logical step explicit. His discovery: when colleagues wrote proofs in structured style, almost every one had serious mistakes — even when the theorems were correct. Conventional proofs hide errors.\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- Structured proofs force each step to be explicitly stated, making it hard to skip justified steps\n- The conventional proof style makes it easy to assert \"this case is similar\" when it isn't\n- When asked to write structured proofs, conjectures that seemed easily provable were often found to be false\n- Skeptical readers who check proofs prefer structured proofs because errors become obvious\n\n---\n\n### 10. \"How to Write Mathematics Badly\" — Jean-Pierre Serre (2003)\n**Transcript (ETH Zurich):** https://metaphor.ethz.ch/x/2025/hs/401-3005-00L/texts/serre_how_to_write_mathematics_badly_transcript.pdf  \n**Video:** Available on YouTube (56-minute Harvard talk)  \n**Blog discussion:** https://golem.ph.utexas.edu/category/2007/02/how_to_write_mathematics_badly.html\n\n**Why it matters:** Serre (Fields Medal, Abel Prize) gives an ironic masterclass by demonstrating what not to do. Extremely specific and practical. Serre was part of Bourbaki and is considered one of the finest mathematical writers.\n\n**Key principles extracted:**\n- Never make implicit identifications between structures — always provide explicit morphisms\n- Name all morphisms; give maps clear names with specified properties\n- Use \"a\" and \"the\" precisely — \"a function\" means any function satisfying conditions; \"the function\" means a canonical specific one\n- Never start a sentence with a symbol — it creates parsing ambiguity\n- Mind basic English grammar — \"its\" vs. \"it's,\" comma usage, subject-verb agreement\n- Define any term that has multiple definitions in the literature — \"twist,\" for example, means different things in different fields\n- Mathematical writing is like language: \"Some mathematicians have a good ear; some not\"\n\n---\n\n## Category B: Philosophy of Mathematics and Scientific Discovery\n\n### 11. \"Proofs and Refutations\" — Imre Lakatos (1976)\n**Publisher:** Cambridge University Press  \n**Internet Archive (restricted borrow):** https://archive.org/details/proofsrefutation0000laka  \n**Partial free text (Cornell):** https://e.math.cornell.edu/people/mann/classes/chicago/Lakatos.pdf\n\n**Why it matters:** The most important philosophical work for anyone presenting a genuinely novel mathematical framework. Lakatos shows that mathematics advances not by accumulation of established truths but through a dynamic process of conjectures, proofs, and counterexamples. Definitions evolve. What counts as a \"proof\" changes. This is both liberating (your framework doesn't need to be final) and strategic (anticipate counterexamples; incorporate them).\n\n**Key ideas:**\n- Mathematics grows through \"proofs and refutations\" — primitive conjecture, rough proof, counterexample, refinement, new conjecture\n- Definitions are not fixed — they must be \"patched up in the light of later insights, in particular failed proofs\"\n- Counterexamples are not failures — they are opportunities to sharpen definitions and discover deeper structure\n- The process: primitive conjecture → rough proof-sketch → global counterexample → locate the \"guilty lemma\" → refine conjecture → new field opens\n- This gives mathematics \"a somewhat experimental flavour\" — it is more like science than its formal presentation suggests\n- Lakatos challenges formalism: informal mathematics is fallible, dynamic, and rational — not the sterile accumulation of certified truths\n- For presenting a new framework: expect your critics to find \"monster-barring\" counterexamples; have refined definitions ready; show how your framework incorporates the most challenging cases\n\n**Full structure of Lakatos's method:**\n1. Primitive conjecture\n2. Proof — a rough thought-experiment decomposing the conjecture into subconjectures\n3. Global counterexamples emerge\n4. The \"guilty lemma\" is located — the step to which the counterexample is local\n5. Previously accepted consequences are checked\n6. Counterexamples become new examples — new fields of inquiry open\n\n---\n\n### 12. \"The Structure of Scientific Revolutions\" — Thomas S. Kuhn (1962)\n**Internet Archive (full text, PDF, EPUB):** https://archive.org/details/thomas-s.-kuhn-the-structure-of-scientific-revolutions  \n**Note:** The 1990 scan on Archive.org is in public domain format with PDF/EPUB download available\n\n**Why it matters:** The foundational text for understanding how paradigm shifts happen and why they are so difficult. Essential for anyone presenting work that challenges existing frameworks.\n\n**Key ideas:**\n- Normal science operates within a \"paradigm\" — a shared framework of assumptions, exemplary problems, and standards\n- Anomalies accumulate: puzzles that normal science cannot solve\n- Crisis emerges when anomalies cannot be ignored or explained away\n- Revolution: a new paradigm that resolves the anomalies — but this involves a gestalt shift, not just adding new facts\n- \"Paradigm shifts aren't logical debates but involve community-wide perspective changes\"\n- After a revolution, the old and new paradigms are \"incommensurable\" — they literally see the world differently\n- **For presenting a new framework:** Document the anomalies in the current paradigm; show your framework resolves them; expect resistance from those heavily invested in the existing paradigm; find early adopters rather than trying to convert true believers\n\n**Chapters:**\n1. Introduction: A Role for History\n2. The Route to Normal Science\n3. The Nature of Normal Science\n4. Normal Science as Puzzle-solving\n5. The Priority of Paradigms\n6. Anomaly and the Emergence of Scientific Discoveries\n7. Crisis and the Emergence of Scientific Theories\n8. The Response to Crisis\n9. The Nature and Necessity of Scientific Revolutions\n10. Revolutions as Changes of World View\n11. The Invisibility of Revolutions\n12. The Resolutions of Revolutions\n13. Progress through Revolutions\n14. Postscript—1969\n\n---\n\n### 13. \"How to Solve It\" — George Polya (1945)\n**Internet Archive (full text):** https://archive.org/details/polya-how-to-solve-it\n\n**Why it matters:** The foundational text on mathematical heuristics — the art of guided discovery. Polya's four-step method is relevant not just to problem-solving but to mathematical presentation: a paper that mirrors the logic of discovery is more persuasive than one that presents a finished proof without context.\n\n**Key principles:**\n- Four-step method: Understand the problem → Devise a plan → Carry out the plan → Look back\n- Heuristic strategies: analogy, specialization, generalization, working backwards, auxiliary elements\n- Mathematical thinking involves \"plausible reasoning\" alongside proof — intuition precedes rigor\n- Good exposition mirrors the process of discovery, not just the final polished result\n- Ask: \"What is the unknown? What are the data? What is the condition?\" — these questions structure both problem-solving and mathematical writing\n\n---\n\n### 14. \"A Mathematician's Apology\" — G.H. Hardy (1940)\n**Free PDF:** https://www.arvindguptatoys.com/arvindgupta/mathsapology-hardy.pdf\n\n**Why it matters:** The canonical defense of mathematical beauty as intrinsic value. Hardy's aesthetic criteria for mathematical work remain the most cited in the literature.\n\n**Key ideas:**\n- \"A mathematician, like a painter or poet, is a maker of patterns. The mathematician's patterns must be beautiful; the ideas must fit together in a harmonious way.\"\n- Beauty in mathematics involves: unexpectedness, inevitability, and economy\n- These three aesthetic criteria apply to papers as well: the result should surprise, the proof should feel inevitable in retrospect, and nothing should be extraneous\n- \"Exposition, criticism, appreciation, is work for second-rate minds\" — Hardy was wrong about this, but understanding his view clarifies why mathematical writing is undervalued and what must overcome that bias\n- Pure mathematics prizes: significance (not triviality), generality, depth, unexpectedness, inevitability, economy\n\n---\n\n## Category C: Academic Writing and Persuasion\n\n### 15. \"The Sense of Style\" — Steven Pinker (2014)\n**Publisher:** Viking/Penguin  \n**Summary:** https://www.themarginalian.org/2014/10/10/steven-pinker-sense-of-style/\n\n**Why it matters:** A cognitive scientist's analysis of why academic writing fails and what makes writing genuinely clear. Applies linguistics and psychology to writing instruction. Particularly useful for understanding why even highly intelligent writers produce unreadable prose.\n\n**Key principles:**\n- Classic style: the writer shows the reader something in the world. Writing is a transparent window, not a display of erudition.\n- \"The curse of knowledge\" — the most common cause of bad writing: writers cannot remember what it was like not to know what they now know, so they omit essential context\n- Writing is an act of \"imagining a reader\" — you must consciously construct a model of who you are writing for\n- Good writers are avid readers — they have internalized examples and can reverse-engineer effective techniques\n- Clarity, trust, beauty — three reasons style matters\n- Avoid \"hedgehog\" academic prose (elaborate hedging, passive voice, jargon walls) in favor of direct assertion backed by evidence\n- Rules exist for reasons — understand the reason to know when to break the rule\n\n---\n\n### 16. \"Stylish Academic Writing\" — Helen Sword (2012)\n**Publisher:** Harvard University Press\n\n**Why it matters:** Sword analyzed over 1,000 peer-reviewed articles and found a startling gap between what academics say good writing looks like and what they actually produce. Based on empirical research.\n\n**Key principles:**\n- \"Elegant ideas deserve elegant expression\"\n- Six universally agreed elements of stylish writing: clarity, coherence, concision; short or mixed-length sentences; plain English; precision; active verbs; telling a story\n- Use anecdotes and carefully chosen metaphors — they are not unscholarly, they are effective\n- Opening sentences should make readers want to continue\n- The myth that complex ideas require complex prose is empirically false — the most cited papers are often the most clearly written\n- \"Jargon walls\" are professional insecurity, not precision\n\n---\n\n### 17. \"Economical Writing\" — Deirdre McCloskey (3rd ed.)\n**Publisher:** University of Chicago Press  \n**Key principles summary:** https://www.paragraphai.com/post/economical-writing-book-rules-by-deirdre-mccloskey\n\n**Why it matters:** 35 pithy rules for writing clear, persuasive academic prose. McCloskey is an economist who writes about writing — her advice is battle-tested in one of the most jargon-dense fields.\n\n**Key principles:**\n- If you write to impress, it will fail — write to communicate\n- The best sentence contains no unnecessary words (builds on Strunk & White)\n- Use active voice and strong verbs\n- Revision is the real work of writing — economical writing results from careful editing, not natural flow\n- Six organizing principles: clarity, brevity, reader engagement, logical structure, revision, integration\n- Write with an eye toward what the reader will take away, not what you want to display\n\n---\n\n### 18. \"The Elements of Style\" — Strunk & White (4th ed.)\n**Free PDF:** https://ia801500.us.archive.org/26/items/pdfy-2_qp8jQ61OI6NHwa/Strunk%20%26%20White%20-%20The%20Elements%20of%20Style,%204th%20Edition.pdf\n\n**Key principles:**\n- Omit needless words — \"Vigorous writing is concise... every word should tell\"\n- Use active voice\n- Put statements in positive form — say what is, not what isn't\n- Choose a suitable design and stick to it\n- Make the paragraph the unit of composition\n- Use definite, specific, concrete language\n- Express coordinate ideas in similar form (parallelism)\n- Place the emphatic words at the end of a sentence\n\n---\n\n## Category D: Ten Rules for Structuring Papers\n\n### 19. \"Ten Simple Rules for Structuring Papers\" — Mensh & Kording (2017)\n**Free full text:** https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC5619685/\n\n**Why it matters:** Empirically grounded rules for structuring papers to maximize persuasiveness and impact. Context-Content-Conclusion structure at every scale.\n\n**Ten rules:**\n1. Focus on one central contribution — papers trying to make multiple points are less convincing about each\n2. Write for naive readers — define terms, minimize cognitive load\n3. Use Context-Content-Conclusion (C-C-C) structure at every level\n4. Optimize logical flow — present related ideas consecutively; use parallel structure\n5. Tell a complete story in the abstract — C-C-C structure; start broad, narrow to the gap, broaden to significance\n6. Communicate why the paper matters in the introduction — establish a gap progressively\n7. Present results as logically connected statements — figures communicate conclusions\n8. Discuss limitations and field relevance in the discussion\n9. Allocate time strategically — titles, abstracts, and figures receive far more attention than methods\n10. Iterate through feedback — treat writing as optimization requiring multiple reviewers\n\n---\n\n## Category E: Presenting Novel Frameworks to Skeptical Audiences\n\n### 20. Lessons from History of Ignored Discoveries (Nintil research)\n**Source:** https://nintil.com/discoveries-ignored/\n\n**Patterns of rejection:**\n- Outsider status (from non-prestigious institution) triggers automatic skepticism\n- Noisy data makes replication difficult, creating lasting doubt\n- Paradigm conflicts: ideas contradicting existing models are dismissed without examination\n- Wrong mechanism, right conclusion: correct finding through flawed reasoning gets rejected entirely\n\n**Practical strategies for new paradigm advocates:**\n- Expect 5-15 journal rejections — mRNA vaccines, CRISPR, PCR all faced this\n- Build diverse funding sources — don't rely on a single grant source\n- Recruit established allies within the field — one established supporter is worth dozens of sympathetic strangers\n- Frame work conservatively at first, expand implications after initial acceptance\n- Replicate findings rigorously — overcome historical skepticism through reproducibility\n- Accept partial credit rather than claiming to revolutionize everything\n- Document early rejection — it becomes validation after success\n\n### 21. Resistance to Revolutionary Theories (ScienceDirect)\n**Pattern from research on revolutionary theory acceptance:**\n- Three stages of resistance: \"not true\" → \"true but unimportant\" → \"true, important, but we knew it all along\"\n- A new theory needs evidence AND explanation — neither alone is sufficient\n- Great ideas sometimes arrive too early — they need new data, technology, or infrastructure to be realized\n- The scientific community appropriately contains both bold and conservative individuals — this is healthy, not hostile\n\n---\n\n## Category F: Advice from Master Mathematical Writers\n\n### 22. Terence Tao's Writing Advice Blog\n**Main page:** https://terrytao.wordpress.com/advice-on-writing-papers/\n\n**Key principles:**\n- Use \"liberal amounts of plain English\" — English communicates on multiple levels beyond what symbols can\n- Use connectors purposefully: \"also/furthermore\" (equal importance), \"therefore/since\" (logical consequence), \"however/but\" (contrast), \"in particular\" (specificity), \"more generally\" (broader scope), \"similarly\" (analogy without deduction)\n- Reserve notation for \"standard formal computations\" where readers already understand the general approach\n- \"The primary purpose of mathematical writing is to communicate and inform, not to impress\"\n- Develop your own individual style rather than mimicking the literature — this preserves intellectual honesty about which insights are yours\n- Write professionally while maintaining your authentic voice\n\n### 23. Quintilian's Principle (via Halmos)\n\"We should not write so that it is possible for our readers to understand us, but so that it is impossible for them to misunderstand us.\"\n\nThis 2,000-year-old standard — cited in Halmos discussions — is the highest bar for mathematical exposition.\n\n---\n\n## Access Summary\n\n| Book | Free Full Text | Source |\n|------|----------------|--------|\n| Halmos
